Betty do look , and her eye open even wide , -- as well they might , -- for there , just where they first put it , be the lost cake , unhurt , unchanged , except that the big B have coast a little further down the gingerbread hill .
CHAPTER II WHERE THEY FOUND HIS MASTER Neither speak for a minute , astonishment be too great for word ; then , a by one impulse , both steal up and touch the cake with a timid finger , quite prepared to see it fly away in some mysterious and startling manner .
It remain sit tranquilly in the basket , however , and the child draw a long breath of relief , for , though they do not believe in fairy , the late performance do seem rather like witchcraft .
`` The dog do n't eat it ! ''
`` Sally do n't take it ! ''
`` How do you know ? ''
`` She never would have put it back . ''
`` Who do ? ''
`` Ca n't tell , but I forgive 'em . ''

ask Betty , feeling a if it would be very difficult to settle down to a quiet tea-party after such unusual excitement .
`` Eat that cake up just as fast a ever we can , '' and Bab divide the contested delicacy with one chop of the big knife , bound to make sure of her own share at all event .
It do not take long , for they wash it down with sip of milk , and ate as fast a possible , glance round all the while to see if the queer dog be come again .
`` There !
now I 'd like to see any one take my cake away , '' say Bab , defiantly crunch her half of the pie-crust B . `` Or mine either , '' cough Betty , choke over a raisin that would n't go down in a hurry .

`` That will be splendid .

`` P ` r ` aps she will , but you need n't begin to sneeze yet awhile .

`` Then who do ?

A yellow-bird sat swinging and chirp on the tall lilac-bush , but no other live thing be in sight .

I do n't like such action , and I 'm afraid to stay . ''

Clearing the table in a summary manner by catch up the four corner of the cloth , Bab put the rattling bundle into her apron , fling her child on the top and pronounce herself ready to depart .

But Mrs. Moss could not keep her promise , for on Monday it still rain , and the little girl paddle off to school like a pair of young duck , enjoy every puddle they come to , since India-rubber boot make wad a delicious possibility .
They take their dinner , and at noon regale a crowd of comrade with an account of the mysterious dog , who appear to be haunt the neighborhood , a several of the other child have see him examine their back yard with interest .
He have beg of them , but to none have he exhibit his accomplishment except Bab and Betty ; and they be therefore much set up , and call him `` our dog '' with an air .
The cake transaction remain a riddle , for Sally Folsom solemnly declare that she be play tag in Mamie Snow 's barn at that identical time .
No one have be near the old house but the two child , and no one could throw any light upon that singular affair .
It produce a great effect , however ; for even `` teacher '' be interested , and tell such amazing tale of a juggler she once saw , that doughnut be leave forget in dinner-baskets , and wedge of pie remain suspend in the air for several minute at a time , instead of vanish with miraculous rapidity a usual .
At afternoon recess , which the girl have first , Bab nearly dislocate every joint of her little body try to imitate the poodle 's antic .
She have practise on her bed with great success , but the wood-shed floor be a different thing , a her knee and elbow soon testify .
`` It look just as easy a any thing ; I do n't see how he do it , '' she say , come down with a bump after vainly attempt to walk on her hand .

`` Oh , do call him in and make him dance ! ''
cry the girl , all chirp at once , till it sound a if a flock of sparrow have take possession of the shed .

He evidently have not forget , however ; for , though he pause and look wistfully at them , he would not approach , but stand drip in the rain , with his frill much bedraggle , while his tasselled tail wag slowly , and his pink nose point suggestively to the pail and basket , nearly empty now .
`` He 's hungry ; give him something to eat , and then he 'll see that we do n't want to hurt him , '' suggest Sally , start a contribution with her last bit of bread and butter .
Bab catch up her new pail , and collect all the odds and end ; then try to beguile the poor beast in to eat and be comfort .
But he only come as far a the door , and , sit up , beg with such implore eye that Bab put down the pail and step back , say pitifully , -- `` The poor thing be starve ; let him eat all he want , and we wo n't touch him . ''
The girl draw back with little cluck of interest and compassion ; but I regret to say their charity be not reward a they expect , for , the minute the coast be clear , the dog march boldly up , seize the handle of the pail in his mouth , and be off with it , gallop down the road at a great pace .
Shrieks arose from the child , especially Bab and Betty , basely bereave of their new dinner-pail ; but no one could follow the thief , for the bell rang , and in they go , so much excite that the boy rush tumultuously forth to discover the cause .
By the time school be over the sun be out , and Bab and Betty hasten home to tell their wrong and be comfort by mother , who do it most effectually .
`` Never mind , dear , I 'll get you another pail , if he do n't bring it back a he do before .
As it be too wet for you to play out , you shall go and see the old coach-house a I promise .
Keep on your rubber and come along . ''
This delightful prospect much assuage their woe , and away they go , skip gayly down the gravelled path , while Mrs. Moss follow , with skirt well tuck up , and a great bunch of key in her hand ; for she live at the Lodge , and have charge of the premise .
The small door of the coach-house be fasten inside , but the large one have a padlock on it ; and this be quickly unfasten , one half swung open , and the little girl run in , too eager and curious even to cry out when they find themselves at last in possession of the long-coveted old carriage .
A dusty , musty concern enough ; but it have a high seat , a door , step that let down , and many other charm which render it most desirable in the eye of child .
Bab make straight for the box and Betty for the door ; but both come tumble down faster than they go up , when from the gloom of the interior come a shrill bark , and a low voice say quickly , `` Down , Sancho !
down ! ''
`` Who be there ? ''
demand Mrs. Moss , in a stern tone , back toward the door with both child cling to her skirt .
The well-known curly white head be pop out of the broken window , and a mild whine seem to say , `` Do n't be alarm , lady ; we wo n't hurt you .
Come out this minute , or I shall have to come and get you , '' call Mrs. Moss , grow very brave all of a sudden a she catch sight of a pair of small , dusty shoe under the coach .
`` Yes , 'm , I 'm come , as fast a I can , '' answer a meek voice , a what appear to be a bundle of rag leap out of the dark , follow by the poodle , who immediately sit down at the bare foot of his owner with a watchful air , a if ready to assault any one who might approach too near .
`` Now , then , who be you , and how do you get here ? ''
ask Mrs. Moss , try to speak sternly , though her motherly eye be already full of pity , a they rest on the forlorn little figure before her .
CHAPTER III BEN `` Please , 'm , my name be Ben Brown , and I 'm travellin ' . ''
`` Where be you go ? ''
`` Anywheres to get work . ''
`` What sort of work can you do ? ''
`` All kind .
I 'm use to horse . ''
`` Bless me !
such a little chap a you ?
`` I 'm twelve , ma'am , and can ride any thing on four leg ; '' and the small boy give a nod that seem to say , `` Bring on your Cruisers .
I 'm ready for 'em . ''
`` Have n't you get any folk ? ''
ask Mrs. Moss , amuse but still anxious , for the sunburnt face be very thin , the eye hollow with hunger or pain , and the ragged figure lean on the wheel a if too weak or weary to stand alone .
`` No , 'm , not of my own ; and the people I be leave with beat me so , I -- run away . ''
The last word seem to bolt out against his will a if the woman 's sympathy irresistibly win the child 's confidence .
`` Then I do n't blame you .
But how do you get here ? ''
`` I be so tired I could n't go any far , and I think the folk up here at the big house would take me in .
But the gate be lock , and I be so discouraged , I jest lay down outside and give up . ''
`` Poor little soul , I do n't wonder , '' say Mrs. Moss , while the child look deeply interested at mention of their gate .
The boy draw a long breath , and his eye begin to twinkle in spite of his forlorn state a he go on , while the dog prick up his ear at mention of his name : -- `` While I be restin ' I hear some one come along inside , and I peek , and saw them little girls playin ' .
The vittles look so nice I could n't help wantin ' 'em ; but I do n't take nothin ' , -- it be Sancho , and he take the cake for me . ''
Bab and Betty give a gasp and star reproachfully at the poodle , who half close his eye with a meek , unconscious look that be very droll .
`` And you make him put it back ? ''
cry Bab .
`` No ; I do it myself .
Got over the gate when you be racin ' after Sancho , and then clim ' up on the porch and hid , '' say the boy with a grin .
`` And you laugh ? ''
ask Bab .
`` Yes . ''
`` And sneeze ? ''

`` Yes . ''

`` Yes ; and you like 'em , do n't you ? ''

What make you hide ? ''

`` How come you here ? ''

The glass be break , and I only pull the nail out .
I have n't do a mite of harm sleepin ' here two night .
I be so tuckered out I could n't go on nohow , though I try a Sunday . ''
`` And come back again ?
`` Yes , 'm ; it be so lonesome in the rain , and this place seem kinder like home , and I could hear 'em talkin ' outside , and Sanch he find vittles , and I be pretty comfortable . ''
`` Well , I never ! ''
ejaculate Mrs. Moss , whisk up a corner of her apron to wipe her eye , for the thought of the poor little fellow alone there for two day and night with no bed but musty straw , no food but the scrap a dog bring him , be too much for her .
`` Do you know what I 'm go to do with you ? ''
she ask , try to look calm and cool , with a great tear run down her wholesome red cheek , and a smile try to break out at the corner of her lip .
`` No , ma'am , and I dunno a I care .
Only do n't be hard on Sanch ; he 's be real good to me , and we 're fond of one another ; ai n't u , old chap ? ''
answer the boy , with his arm around the dog 's neck , and an anxious look which he have not wear for himself .
`` I 'm go to take you right home , and wash and feed and put you in a good bed ; and to-morrow , -- well , we 'll see what 'll happen then , '' say Mrs. Moss , not quite sure about it herself .
`` You 're very kind , ma'am , I 'll be glad to work for you .
Ai n't you get a horse I can see to ? ''
ask the boy , eagerly .
`` Nothing but hen and a cat . ''
Bab and Betty burst out laugh when their mother say that , and Ben give a faint giggle , a if he would like to join in if he only have the strength to do it .
But his legs shook under him , and he felt a queer dizziness ; so he could only hold on to Sancho , and blink at the light like a young owl .
`` Come right along , child .
Run on , girl , and put the rest of the broth to warming , and fill the kettle .
I 'll see to the boy , '' command Mrs. Moss , wave off the child , and go up to feel the pulse of her new charge , for it suddenly occur to her that he might be sick and not safe to take home .
The hand he give her be very thin , but clean and cool , and the black eye be clear though hollow , for the poor lad be half-starved .
`` I 'm awful shabby , but I ai n't dirty .
I have a washin ' in the rain last night , and I 've jest about live on water lately , '' he explain , wonder why she look at him so hard .
`` Put out your tongue . ''
He do so , but take it in again to say quickly , -- `` I ai n't sick , -- I 'm only hungry ; for I have n't have a mite but what Sanch bring , for three day ; and I always go half , do n't I , Sanch ? ''
The poodle give a shrill bark , and vibrate excitedly between the door and his master a if he understand all that be go on , and recommend a speedy march toward the promise food and shelter .
Mrs. Moss take the hint , and bid the boy follow her at once and bring his `` thing '' with him .
`` I ai n't get any .
Some big feller take away my bundle , else I would n't look so bad .
There 's only this .
I 'm sorry Sanch take it , and I 'd like to give it back if I know whose it be , '' say Ben , bring the new dinner-pail out from the depth of the coach where he have go to housekeep .
`` That 's soon do ; it 's mine , and you 're welcome to the bit your queer dog run off with .
Come along , I must lock up , '' and Mrs. Moss clank her key suggestively .
Ben limp out , lean on a broken hoe-handle , for he be stiff after two day in such damp lodging , as well a wear out with a fortnight 's wandering through sun and rain .
Sancho be in great spirit , evidently feel that their woe be over and his foraging expedition at an end , for he frisk about his master with yelp of pleasure , or make playful dart at the ankle of his benefactress , which cause her to cry , `` Whish ! ''
and `` Scat ! ''
and shake her skirt at him a if he be a cat or hen .
A hot fire be roar in the stove under the broth-skillet and tea-kettle , and Betty be poke in more wood , with a great smirch of black on her chubby cheek , while Bab be cut away at the loaf a if bent on slice her own finger off .
Before Ben know what he be about , he find himself in the old rocking-chair devour bread and butter a only a hungry boy can , with Sancho close by gnaw a mutton-bone like a ravenous wolf in sheep 's clothing .
While the new-comers be thus happily employ , Mrs. Moss beckon the little girl out of the room , and give them both an errand .
`` Bab , you run over to Mrs. Barton 's , and ask her for any old dud Billy do n't want ; and Betty , you go to the Cutters , and tell Miss Clarindy I 'd like a couple of the shirt we make at last sewing circle .
Any shoe , or a hat , or sock , would come handy , for the poor dear have n't a whole thread on him . ''
Away go the child full of anxiety to clothe their beggar ; and so well do they plead his cause with the good neighbor , that Ben hardly know himself when he emerge from the back bedroom half an hour later , clothe in Billy Barton 's faded flannel suit , with an unbleached cotton shirt out of the Dorcas basket , and a pair of Milly Cutter 's old shoe on his foot .
Sancho also have be put in good trim , for , after his master have refresh himself with a warm bath , he give his dog a good scrub while Mrs. Moss set a stitch here and there in the new old clothes ; and Sancho reappear , look more like the china poodle than ever , be as white a snow , his curl well brush up , and his tasselly tail wave proudly over his back .
Feeling eminently respectable and comfortable , the wanderer humbly present themselves , and be greet with smile of approval from the little girl and a hospitable welcome from the mother , who set them near the stove to dry , a both be decidedly damp after their ablution .
`` I declare I should n't have know you ! ''
exclaim the good woman , survey the boy with great satisfaction ; for , though still very thin and tired , the lad have a tidy look that please her , and a lively way of move about in his clothes , like an eel in a skin rather too big for him .
The merry black eye seem to see every thing , the voice have an honest sound , and the sunburnt face look several year young since the unnatural despondency have go out of it .
`` It 's very nice , and me and Sanch be lot oblige , ma'am , '' murmur Ben , get red and bashful under the three pair of friendly eye fix upon him .
Bab and Betty be do up the tea-things with unusual despatch , so that they might entertain their guest , and just a Ben speak Bab drop a cup .
To her great surprise no smash follow , for , bend quickly , the boy catch it a it fell , and present it to her on the back of his hand with a little bow .
`` Gracious !
how could you do it ? ''
ask Bab , look a if she think there be magic about .
`` That 's nothing ; look here , '' and , take two plate , Ben send them spin up into the air , catch and throw so rapidly that Bab and Betty stand with their mouth open , a if to swallow the plate should they fall , while Mrs. Moss , with her dish-cloth suspend , watch the antic of her crockery with a housewife 's anxiety .
`` That do beat all ! ''
be the only exclamation she have time to make ; for , a if desirous of show his gratitude in the only way he could , Ben take clothes-pins from a basket near by , send several saucer twirl up , catch them on the pin , balance the pin on chin , nose , forehead , and go walk about with a new and peculiar sort of toadstool ornament his countenance .
The child be immensely tickle , and Mrs. Moss be so amused she would have lent her best soup-tureen if he have express a wish for it .
But Ben be too tired to show all his accomplishment at once , and he soon stop , look a if he almost regret have betray that he possess any .
`` I guess you 've be in the juggling business , '' say Mrs. Moss , with a wise nod , for she saw the same look on his face a when he say his name be Ben Brown , -- the look of one who be not tell the whole truth .
`` Yes , 'm .
I use to help Senor Pedro , the Wizard of the World , and I learn some of his trick , '' stammer Ben , try to seem innocent .
`` Now , look here , boy , you 'd better tell me the whole story , and tell it true , or I shall have to send you up to judge Morris .
I would n't like to do that , for he be a harsh sort of a man ; so , if you have n't do any thing bad , you need n't be afraid to speak out , and I 'll do what I can for you , '' say Mrs. Moss , rather sternly , a she go and sit down in her rocking-chair , a if about to open the court .
`` I have n't do any thing bad , and I ai n't afraid , only I do n't want to go back ; and if I tell , may be you 'll let 'em know where I be , '' say Ben , much distress between his longing to confide in his new friend and his fear of his old enemy .
`` If they abuse you , of course I would n't .
Tell the truth , and I 'll stand by you .
Girls , you go for the milk . ''
`` Oh , Ma , do let u stay !
We 'll never tell , truly , truly ! ''
cry Bab and Betty , full of dismay be send off when secret be about to be divulge .
`` I do n't mind 'em , '' say Ben handsomely .
`` Very well , only hold your tongue .
Now , boy where do you come from ? ''
say Mrs. Moss , a the little girl hastily sit down together on their private and particular bench opposite their mother , brim with curiosity and beam with satisfaction at the prospect before them .
CHAPTER IV HIS STORY `` I run away from a circus , '' begin Ben , but get no further , for Bab and Betty give a simultaneous bounce of delight , and both cry out at once , -- `` We 've be to one !
It be splendid ! ''
`` You would n't think so if you know as much about it a I do , '' answer Ben , with a sudden frown and wriggle , a if he still felt the smart of the blow he have receive .
`` We do n't call it splendid ; do we , Sancho ? ''
he add , make a queer noise , which cause the poodle to growl and bang the floor irefully with his tail , a he lay close to his master 's foot , get acquaint with the new shoe they wear .
`` How come you there ? ''
ask Mrs. Moss , rather disturb at the news .
`` Why , my father be the ` Wild Hunter of the Plains . '
Did n't you ever see or hear of him ? ''
say Ben , a if surprised at her ignorance .
`` Bless your heart , child , I have n't be to a circus this ten year , and I 'm sure I do n't remember what or who I saw then , '' answer Mrs. Moss , amuse , yet touch by the son 's evident admiration for his father .
`` Did n't you see him ? ''
demand Ben , turn to the little girl .
`` We saw Indians and tumble men , and the Bounding Brothers of Borneo , and a clown and monkey , and a little mite of a pony with blue eye .
Was he any of them ? ''
answer Betty , innocently .
`` Pooh !
he do n't belong to that lot .
He always ride two , four , six , eight horse to oncet , and I use to ride with him till I get too big .
My father be A No . 1 , and do n't do any thing but break horse and ride 'em , '' say Ben , with as much pride a if his parent have be a President .
`` Is he dead ? ''
ask Mrs. Moss .
`` I do n't know .
Wish I do , '' -- and poor Ben give a gulp a if something rise in his throat and choke him .
`` Tell u all about it , dear , and may be we can find out where he be , '' say Mrs. Moss , lean forward to pat the shiny dark head that be suddenly bent over the dog .
`` Yes , ma'am .
I will , thank y ' , '' and with an effort the boy steady his voice and plunge into the middle of his story .
`` Father be always good to me , and I like bein ' with him after granny die .
I live with her till I be seven ; then father take me , and I be train for rider .
You jest oughter have see me when I be a little feller all in white tights , and a gold belt , and pink riggin ' , stand ' on father 's shoulder , or hangin ' on to old General 's tail , and him gallopin ' full pelt ; or father ridin ' three horse with me on his head wavin ' flag , and every one clapping like fun . ''
`` Oh , be n't you scar to piece ? ''
ask Betty , quake at the mere thought .
`` Not a bit .
I like it . ''
`` So should I ! ''
cry Bab enthusiastically .
`` Then I drive the four pony in the little chariot , when we parade , '' continue Ben , `` and I sit on the great ball up top of the grand car draw by Hannibal and Nero .
But I do n't like that , 'cause it be awful high and shaky , and the sun be hot , and the tree slap my face , and my leg ache holdin ' on . ''
`` What 's hanny bell and neroes ? ''
demand Betty .
`` Big elephant .
Father never let 'em put me up there , and they do n't darst till he be go ; then I have to , else they 'd ` a ' thrash me . ''
`` Did n't any one take your part ? ''
ask Mrs. Moss .
`` Yes , 'm , ` most all the lady do ; they be very good to me , ` specially ` Melia .
She vow she would n't go on in the Tunnymunt act if they do n't stop knockin ' me round when I would n't help old Buck with the bear .
So they have to stop it , 'cause she lead first rate , and none of the other lady ride half as well a ` Melia . ''
`` Bears !
oh , do tell about them ! ''
exclaim Bab , in great excitement , for at the only circus she have see the animal be her delight .
`` Buck have five of 'em , cross old feller , and he show 'em off .
I play with 'em once , jest for fun , and he think it would make a hit to have me show off instead of him .
But they have a way of clawin ' and huggin ' that be n't nice , and you could n't never tell whether they be good-natured or ready to bite your head off .
Buck be all over scar where they 'd scratch and bite him , and I be n't go to do it ; and I do n't have to , owin ' to Miss St. John 's standin ' by me like a good one . ''
`` Who be Miss St. John ? ''
ask Mrs. Moss , rather confuse by the sudden introduction of new name and people .
`` Why she be ` Melia , -- Mrs. Smithers , the ringmaster 's wife .
His name be n't Montgomery any more 'n hers be St. John .
They all change 'em to something fine on the bill , you know .
Father use to be Senor Jose Montebello ; and I be Master Adolphus Bloomsbury , after I stop bein ' a flyin ' Coopid and a infant Progidy . ''
Mrs. Moss lean back in her chair to laugh at that , greatly to the surprise of the little girl , who be much impress with the elegance of these high-sounding name .
`` Go on with your story , Ben , and tell why you run away and what become of your Pa , '' she say , compose herself to listen , really interested in the child .
`` Well , you see , father have a quarrel with old Smithers , and go off sudden last fall , just before tent season ' be over .
He tell me he be goin ' to a great ridin ' school in New York and when he be fix he 'd send for me .
I be to stay in the museum and help Pedro with the trick business .
He be a nice man and I like him , and ` Melia be goin ' to see to me , and I do n't mind for awhile .
But father do n't send for me , and I begin to have horrid time .
If it have n't be for ` Melia and Sancho I would have cut away long before I do . ''
`` What do you have to do ? ''
`` Lots of thing , for time be dull and I be smart .
Smithers say so , any way , and I have to tumble up lively when he give the word .
I do n't mind doin ' trick or showin ' off Sancho , for father train him , and he always do well with me .
But they want me to drink gin to keep me small , and I would n't , 'cause father do n't like that kind of thing .
I use to ride tip-top , and that just suit me till I get a fall and hurt my back ; but I have to go on all the same , though I ache dreadful , and use to tumble off , I be so dizzy and weak . ''
`` What a brute that man must have be !
Why do n't ` Melia put a stop to it ? ''
ask Mrs. Moss , indignantly .
`` She die , ma'am , and then there be no one leave but Sanch ; so I run away . ''
Then Ben fell to pat his dog again , to hide the tear he could not keep from come at the thought of the kind friend he have lose .
`` What do you mean to do ? ''
`` Find father ; but I could n't , for he be n't at the ridin ' school , and they tell me he have go out West to buy mustang for a man who want a lot .
So then I be in a fix , for I could n't go to father , do n't know jest where he be , and I would n't sneak back to Smithers to be abuse .
Tried to make 'em take me at the ridin ' school , but they do n't want a boy , and I travel along and try to get work .
But I 'd have starve if it have n't be for Sanch .
I leave him tie up when I run off , for fear they 'd say I steal him .
He 's a very valuable dog , ma'am , the best trick dog I ever see , and they 'd want him back more than they would me .
He belong to father , and I hat to leave him ; but I do .
I hook it one dark night , and never think I 'd see him ag ` in .
Next mornin ' I be eatin ' breakfast in a barn mile away , and dreadful lonesome , when he come tearin ' in , all mud and wet , with a great piece of rope draggin ' .
He 'd gnaw it and come after me , and would n't go back or be lose ; and I 'll never leave him again , will I , dear old feller ? ''
Sancho have listen to this portion of the tale with intense interest , and when Ben speak to him he stand straight up , put both paw on the boy 's shoulder , lick his face with a world of dumb affection in his yellow eye , and give a little whine which say a plainly a word , -- `` Cheer up , little master ; father may vanish and friend die , but I never will desert you . ''
Ben hug him close and smile over his curly , white head at the little girl , who clap their hand at the pleasing tableau , and then go to pat and fondle the good creature , assure him that they entirely forgive the theft of the cake and the new dinner-pail .
Inspired by these endearment and certain private signal give by Ben , Sancho suddenly burst away to perform all his best antic with unusual grace and dexterity .
Bab and Betty dance about the room with rapture , while Mrs. Moss declare she be almost afraid to have such a wonderfully intelligent animal in the house .
Praises of his dog please Ben more than praise of himself , and when the confusion have subside he entertain his audience with a lively account of Sancho 's cleverness , fidelity , and the various adventure in which he have nobly bear his part .
While he talk , Mrs. Moss be make up her mind about him , and when he come to an end of his dog 's perfection , she say , gravely , -- `` If I can find something for you to do , would you like to stay here awhile ? ''
`` Oh , yes , ma'am , I 'd be glad to ! ''
answer Ben , eagerly ; for the place seem home-like already , and the good woman almost as motherly a the departed Mrs. Smithers .
`` Well , I 'll step over to the Squire 's to-morrow to see what he say .
Should n't wonder if he 'd take you for a chore-boy , if you be as smart a you say .
He always have one in the summer , and I have n't see any round yet .
Can you drive cow ? ''
`` Hope so ; '' and Ben give a shrug , a if it be a very unnecessary question to put to a person who have drive four calico pony in a gilded chariot .
`` It may n't be as lively a rid elephant and play with bear , but it be respectable ; and I guess you 'll be happy switch Brindle and Buttercup than be switch yourself , '' say Mrs. Moss , shake her head at him with a smile .
`` I guess I will , ma'am , '' answer Ben , with sudden meekness , remember the trial from which he have escape .
Very soon after this , he be send off for a good night 's sleep in the back bedroom , with Sancho to watch over him .
But both find it difficult to slumber till the racket overhead subside ; for Bab insist on play she be a bear and devour poor Betty , in spite of her wail , till their mother come up and put an end to it by threaten to send Ben and his dog away in the morning , if the girl `` do n't behave and be as still a mouse . ''
This they solemnly promise ; and they be soon dream of gilded car and mouldy coach , runaway boy and dinner-pails , dance dog and twirl teacup .
CHAPTER V BEN GETS A PLACE When Ben awake next morning , he look about him for a moment half bewilder , because there be neither a canvas tent , a barn roof , nor the blue sky above him , but a neat white ceiling , where several fly buzz sociably together , while from without come , not the tramping of horse , the twitter of swallow , or the chirp of early bird , but the comfortable cackle of hen and the sound of two little voice chant the multiplication table .
Sancho sit at the open window , watch the old cat wash her face , and try to imitate her with his great ruffled paw , so awkwardly that Ben laugh ; and Sanch , to hide his confusion at be catch , make one bound from chair to bed , and lick his master 's face so energetically that the boy dive under the bedclothes to escape from the rough tongue .
A rap on the floor from below make both jump up , and in ten minute a shiny-faced lad and a lively dog go race downstairs , -- one to say , `` Good-mornin ' , ma'am , '' the other to wag his tail faster than ever tail wag before , for ham frizzle on the stove , and Sancho be fond of it .
`` Did you rest well ? ''
ask Mrs. Moss , nod at him , fork in hand .
`` Guess I do !
Never saw such a bed .
I 'm use to hay and a horse-blanket , and lately nothin ' but sky for a cover and grass for my feather-bed , '' laugh Ben , grateful for present comfort and make light of past hardship .
`` Clean , sweet corn-husks ai n't bad for young bone , even if they have n't get more flesh on them than yours have , '' answer Mrs. Moss , give the smooth head a motherly stroke a she go by .
`` Fat ai n't allow in our profession , ma'am .
The thinner the good for tight-ropes and tumblin ' ; likewise bareback ridin ' and spry jugglin ' .
Muscle 's the thing , and there you be . ''
Ben stretch out a wiry little arm with a clenched fist at the end of it , a if he be a young Hercules , ready to play ball with the stove if she give him leave .
Glad to see him in such good spirit , she point to the well outside , say pleasantly , -- `` Well , then , just try your muscle by bring in some fresh water . ''
Ben catch up a pail and run off , ready to be useful ; but , while he wait for the bucket to fill down among the mossy stone , he look about him , well pleased with all he saw , -- the small brown house with a pretty curl of smoke rise from it chimney , the little sister sit in the sunshine , green hill and newly-planted field far and near , a brook dance through the orchard , bird sing in the elm avenue , and all the world a fresh and lovely a early summer could make it .
`` Do n't you think it 's pretty nice here ? ''
ask Bab , a his eye come back to them after a long look , which seem to take in every thing , brighten a it rove .
`` Just the nice place that ever be .
Only need a horse round somewhere to be complete , '' answer Ben , a the long well-sweep come up with a drip bucket at one end , an old grindstone at the other .
`` The judge have three , but he 's so fussy about them he wo n't even let u pull a few hair out of old Major 's tail to make ring of , '' say Betty , shut her arithmetic , with an injured expression .
`` Mike let me ride the white one to water when the judge be n't round .
It 's such fun to go jounce down the lane and back .
I do love horse ! ''
cry Bab , bob up and down on the blue bench to imitate the motion of white Jenny .
`` I guess you be a plucky sort of a girl , '' and Ben give her an approving look a he go by , take care to slop a little water on Mrs. Puss , who stand curl her whisker and hump up her back at Sancho .
`` Come to breakfast ! ''
call Mrs. Moss ; and for about twenty minute little be say , a mush and milk vanish in a way that would have astonish even Jack the Giant-killer with his leather bag .
`` Now , girl , fly round and get your chore do up ; Ben , you go chop me some kindling ; and I 'll make thing tidy .
Then we can all start off at once , '' say Mrs. Moss , a the last mouthful vanish , and Sancho lick his lip over the savory scrap that fell to his share .
Ben fell to chop so vigorously that chip fly wildly all about the shed ; Bab rattle the cup into her dish-pan with dangerous haste , and Betty raise a cloud of dust `` sweeping-up ; '' while mother seem to be everywhere at once .
Even Sanch , feel that his fate be at stake , endeavor to help in his own somewhat erratic way , -- now frisk about Ben at the risk of get his tail chop off , then trot away to poke his inquisitive nose into every closet and room whither he follow Mrs. Moss in her `` fly round '' evolution ; next dragging off the mat so Betty could brush the door-steps , or inspect Bab 's dish-washing by stand on his hind-legs to survey the table with a critical air .
When they drive him out he be not the least offended , but gayly bark Puss up a tree , chase all the hen over the fence , and carefully inter an old shoe in the garden , where the remains of the mutton-bone be already bury .
By the time the others be ready , he have work off his superfluous spirit , and trot behind the party like a well-behaved dog accustom to go out walk with lady .
At the cross-roads they separate , the little girl run on to school , while Mrs. Moss and Ben go up to the Squire 's big house on the hill .
`` Do n't you be scar , child .
I 'LL make it all right about your run away ; and if the Squire give you a job , just thank him for it , and do your best to be steady and industrious ; then you 'll get on , I have n't a doubt , '' she whisper , ring the Ben at a side-door , on which the word `` Morris '' shone in bright letter .
`` Come in ! ''
call a gruff voice ; and , feel very much a if he be go to have a tooth out , Ben meekly follow the good woman , who put on her pleasant smile , anxious to make the best possible impression .
A white-headed old gentleman sit read a paper , and peer over his glass at the new-comers with a pair of sharp eye , say in a testy tone , which would have rather daunt any one who do not know what a kind heart he have under his capacious waistcoat , -- `` Good-morning , ma'am .
What 's the matter now ?
Young tramp be steal your chicken ? ''
`` Oh , dear no , sir ! ''
exclaim Mrs. Moss , a if shock at the idea .
Then , in a few word , she tell Ben 's story , unconsciously make his wrong and destitution so pathetic by her look and tone , that the Squire could not help be interested , and even Ben pity himself a if he be somebody else .
`` Now , then , boy , what can you do ? ''
ask the old gentleman , with an approve nod to Mrs. Moss a she finish , and such a keen glance from under his bushy brows that Ben felt a if he be perfectly transparent . ''
` Most any thing , sir , to get my livin ' . ''
`` Can you weed ? ''
`` Never do , but I can learn , sir . ''
`` Pull up all the beet and leave the pigweed , hey ?
Can you pick strawberry ? ''
`` Never try any thing but eatin ' 'em , sir , '' `` Not likely to forget that part of the job .
Can you ride a horse to plow ? ''
`` Guess I could , sir ! ''
-- and Ben 's eye begin to sparkle , for he dearly love the noble animal who have be his dear friend lately .
`` No antic allow .
My horse be a fine fellow , and I 'm very particular about him . ''
The Squire speak soberly , but there be a twinkle in his eye , and Mrs. Moss try not to smile ; for the Squire 's horse be a joke all over the town , be about twenty year old , and have a peculiar gait of his own , lift his fore-feet very high , with a great show of speed , though never go out of a jog-trot .
The boy use to say he gallop before and walk behind , and make all sort of fun of the big , Roman-nosed beast , who allow no liberty to be take with him .
`` I 'm too fond of horse to hurt 'em , Sir .
As for ridin ' , I ai n't afraid of any thing on four leg .
The King of Morocco use to kick and bite like fun , but I could manage him first-rate . ''
`` Then you 'd be able to drive cow to pasture , perhaps ? ''
`` I 've drive elephant and camel , ostrich and grizzly bear , and mule , and six yellow pony all to oncet .
May be I could manage cow if I try hard , '' answer Ben , endeavor to be meek and respectful when scorn fill his soul at the idea of not be able to drive a cow .
The Squire like him all the good for the droll mixture of indignation and amusement betray by the fire in his eye and the sly smile round his lip ; and be rather tickle by Ben 's list of animal , he answer gravely , -- `` Do n't raise elephant and camel much round here .
Bears use to be plenty , but folk get tire of them .
Mules be numerous , but we have the two-legged kind ; and a a general thing prefer Shanghae fowl to ostrich . ''
He get no farther , for Ben laugh out so infectiously that both the others join him ; and somehow that jolly laugh seem to settle matter than word .
As they stop , the Squire tap on the window behind him , say , with an attempt at the former gruffness , -- `` We 'll try you on cow awhile .
My man will show you where to drive them , and give you some odd job through the day .
I 'll see what you be good for , and send you word to-night , Mrs. Moss .
The boy can sleep at your house , ca n't he ? ''
`` Yes , indeed , sir .
He can go on do it , and come up to his work just as well a not .
I can see to him then , and he wo n't be a care to any one , '' say Mrs. Moss , heartily .
`` I 'll make inquiry concern your father , boy ; meantime mind what you be about , and have a good report to give when he come for you , '' return the Squire , with a warn wag of a stern fore-finger .
`` Thanky ' , sir .
I will , sir .
Father 'll come just as soon a he can , if he be n't sick or lose , '' murmur Ben , inwardly thank his star that he have not do any thing to make him quake before that awful finger , and resolve that he never would .
Here a red-headed Irishman come to the door , and stand eye the boy with small favor while the Squire give his order .
`` Pat , this lad want work .
He 's to take the cow and go for them .
Give him any light job you have , and let me know if he 's good for any thing . ''
`` Yis , your honor .
Come out o ' this , b ` y , till I show ye the baste , '' respond Pat ; and , with a hasty good-by to Mrs. Moss , Ben follow his new leader , sorely tempt to play some naughty trick upon him in return for his ungracious reception .
But in a moment he forget that Pat exist , for in the yard stand the Duke of Wellington , so name in honor of his Roman nose .
If Ben have know any thing about Shakespeare , he would have cry , `` A horse , a horse !
my kingdom for a horse ! ''
for the feeling be in his heart , and he run up to the stately animal without a fear .
Duke put back his ear and swish his tail a if displease for a moment ; but Ben look straight in his eye , give a scientific stroke to the iron-gray nose , and utter a chirrup which make the ear prick up a if recognize a familiar sound .
`` He 'll nip ye , if ye go botherin ' that way .
Leave him alone , and attend to the cattle a his honor tell ye , '' command Pat , who make a great show of respect toward Duke in public , and kick him brutally in private .
`` I ai n't afraid !
You wo n't hurt me , will you , old feller ?
See there now !
-- he know I 'm a friend , and take to me right off , '' say Ben , with an arm around Duke 's neck , and his own cheek confidingly lay against the animal 's ; for the intelligent eye speak to him as plainly a the little whinny which he understand and accept a a welcome .
The Squire saw it all from the open window , and suspect from Pat 's face that trouble be brewing , call out , -- `` Let the lad harness Duke , if he can .
I 'm go out directly , and he may as well try that a any thing . ''
Ben be delight , and prove himself so brisk and handy that the roomy chaise stand at the door in a surprisingly short time , with a smile little ostler at Duke 's head when the Squire come out .
His affection for the horse please the old gentleman , and his neat way of harness suit a well ; but Ben get no praise , except a nod and a brief `` All right , boy , '' a the equipage go creak and jog away .
Four sleek cow file out of the barnyard when Pat open the gate , and Ben drive them down the road to a distant pasture where the early grass await their eager cropping .
By the school they go , and the boy look pityingly at the black , brown , and yellow head bob past the window a a class go up to recite ; for it seem a hard thing to the liberty-loving lad to be shut up there so many hour on a morning like that .
But a little breeze that be play truant round the step do Ben a service without know it , for a sudden puff blow a torn leaf to his foot , and see a picture he take it up .
It evidently have fall from some ill-used history , for the picture show some queer ship at anchor , some oddly dress men just land , and a crowd of Indians dance about on the shore .
Ben spell out all he could about these interesting personage , but could not discover what it mean , because ink evidently have deluge the page , to the new reader 's great disappointment .
`` I 'll ask the girl ; may be they will know , '' say Ben to himself a , after look vainly for more stray leaf , he trudge on , enjoy the bobolink 's song , the warm sunshine , and a comfortable sense of friendliness and safety , which soon set him to whistle as gayly a any blackbird in the meadow .
CHAPTER VI A CIRCULATING LIBRARY After supper that night , Bab and Betty sit in the old porch playing with Josephus and Belinda , and discuss the event of the day ; for the appearance of the strange boy and his dog have be a most exciting occurrence in their quiet life .
They have see nothing of him since morning , a he take his meal at the Squire 's , and be at work with Pat in a distant field when the child pass .
Sancho have stick closely to his master , evidently rather bewilder by the new order of thing , and bound to see that no harm happen to Ben .
`` I wish they 'd come .
It 's sundown , and I hear the cow mooing , so I know they have go home , '' say Betty , impatiently ; for she regard the new-comer in the light of an entertaining book , and wish to read on as fast a possible .
`` I 'm go to learn the sign he make when he want Sancho to dance ; then we can have fun with him whenever we like .
He 's the dear dog I ever saw ! ''
answer Bab , who be fonder of animal than her sister .
`` Ma say -- Ow , what 's that ? ''
cry Betty with a start , a something bump against the gate outside ; and in a moment Ben 's head peep over the top a he swing himself up to the iron arch , in the middle of which be the empty lantern frame .
`` Please to locate , gentleman ; please to locate .
The performance be about to begin with the great Flyin ' Coopid act , in which Master Bloomsbury have appear before the crowned head of Europe .
Pronounced by all beholder the most remarkable youthful progidy agoin ' .
Hooray !
here we be ! ''
Having rattle off the familiar speech in Mr. Smithers 's elegant manner , Ben begin to cut up such caper that even a party of dignified hen , go down the avenue to bed , pause to look on with cluck of astonishment , evidently fancy that salt have set him to flutter and tumble a it do them .
Never have the old gate behold such antic , though it have see gay doings in it time ; for of all the boy who have climb over it , not one have ever stand on his head upon each of the big ball which ornament the post , hung by his heel from the arch , go round and round like a wheel with the bar for an axis , play a tattoo with his toe while hold on by his chin , walk about the wall on his hand , or close the entertainment by festoon himself in an airy posture over the side of the lantern frame , and kiss his hand to the audience a a well-bred Cupid be suppose to do on make his bow .
The little girl clap and stamp enthusiastically , while Sancho , who have be calmly survey the show , bark his approval a he leap up to snap at Ben 's foot .
`` Come down and tell what you do up at the Squire 's .
Was he cross ?
Did you have to work hard ?
Do you like it ? ''
ask Bab , when the noise have subside .
`` It 's cool up here , '' answer Ben , compose himself in the frame , and fan his hot face with a green spray broken from the tall bush rustle odorously all about him .
`` I do all sort of job .
The old gentleman be n't cross ; he give me a dime , and I like him first-rate .
But I just hate ` Carrots ; ' he swear at a feller , and fire a stick of wood at me .
Guess I 'll pay him off when I get a chance . ''
Fumbling in his pocket to show the bright dime , he find the torn page , and remember the thirst for information which have seize him in the morning .
`` Look here , tell me about this , will you ?
What be these chap up to ?
The ink have spoil all but the picture and this bit of reading .
I want to know what it mean .
Take it to 'em , Sanch . ''
The dog catch the leaf a it flutter to the ground , and carry it carefully in his mouth , deposit it at the foot of the little girl , seat himself before them with an air of deep interest .
Bab and Betty pick it up and read it aloud in unison , while Ben lean from his perch to listen and learn . ''
` When day dawn , land be visible .
A pleasant land it be .
There be gay flower , and tall tree with leaf and fruit , such a they have never see before .
On the shore be unclad copper-colored men , gaze with wonder at the Spanish ship .
They take them for great bird , the white sail for their wing , and the Spaniards for superior being bring down from heaven on their back . ''
`` Why , that 's Columbus find San Salvador .
Do n't you know about him ? ''
demand Bab , a if she be one of the `` superior being , '' and intimately acquaint with the immortal Christopher .
`` No , I do n't .
Who be he any way ?
I s ` pose that 's him paddlin ' ahead ; but which of the Injuns be Sam Salvindoor ? ''
ask Ben , rather ashamed of his ignorance , but bent on find out now he have begin .
`` My gracious !
twelve year old and not know your Quackenbos ! ''
laugh Bab , much amuse , but rather glad to find that she could teach the `` whirligig boy '' something , for she consider him a remarkable creature .
`` I do n't care a bit for your quackin ' bos , whoever he be .
Tell about this fine feller with the ship ; I like him , '' persist Ben .
So Bab , with frequent interruption and hint from Betty , tell the wonderful tale in a simple way , which make it easy to understand ; for she like history , and have a lively tongue of her own .
`` I 'd like to read some more .
Would my ten cent buy a book ? ''
ask Ben , anxious to learn a little since Bab laugh at him .
`` No , indeed !
I 'll lend you mine when I 'm not use it , and tell you all about it , '' promise Bab ; forget that she do not know `` all about it '' herself yet .
`` I do n't have any time only evening , and then may be you 'll want it , '' begin Ben , in whom the inky page have rouse a strong curiosity .
`` I do get my history in the evening , but you could have it morning before school . ''
`` I shall have to go off early , so there wo n't be any chance .
Yes , there will , -- I 'LL tell you how to do it .
Let me read while I drive up the cow .
Squire like 'em to eat slow along the road , so 's to keep the grass short and save mowin ' .
Pat say so , and I could do history instead of loafin ' round ! ''
cry Ben full of this bright idea .
`` How will I get my book back in time to recite ? ''
ask Bab , prudently .
`` Oh , I 'll leave it on the window-sill , or put it inside the door a I go back .
I 'll be real careful , and just as soon a I earn enough , I 'll buy you a new one and take the old one .
Will you ? ''
`` Yes ; but I 'll tell you a nice way to do .
Do n't put the book on the window , 'cause teacher will see you ; or inside the door , 'cause some one may steal it .
You put it in my cubby-house , right at the corner of the wall near the big maple .
You 'll find a cunning place between the root that stick up under the flat stone .
That 's my closet , and I keep thing there .
It 's the best cubby of all , and we take turn to have it . ''
`` I 'll find it , and that 'll be a first-rate place , '' say Ben , much gratify .
`` I could put my reading-book in sometimes , if you 'd like it .
There 's lot of pretty story in it and picture , '' propose Betty , rather timidly ; for she want to share the benevolent project , but have little to offer , not be a good a scholar a Bab .
`` I 'd like a ` rithmetic better .
I read tip-top , but I ai n't much on ` rithmetic ; so , if you can spare yours , I might take a look at it .
Now I 'm goin ' to earn wage , I ought to know about addin ' 'em up , and so on , '' say Ben , with the air of a Vanderbilt oppress with the care of million .
`` I 'll teach you that .
Betty do n't know much about sum .
But she spell splendidly , and be always at the head of her class .
Teacher be real proud of her , 'cause she never miss , and spell hard , fussy word , like chi-rog-ra-phy and bron-chi-tis as easy a any thing . ''
Bab quite beam with sisterly pride , and Betty smooth down her apron with modest satisfaction , for Bab seldom praise her , and she like it very much .
`` I never go to school , so that 's the reason I ai n't smart .
I can write , though , good 'n some of the boy up at school .
I saw lot of name on the shed door .
See here , now , '' -- and scramble down , Ben pull out a cherished bit of chalk , and flourish off ten letter of the alphabet , one on each of the dark stone slab that pave the walk .
`` Those be beautiful !
I ca n't make such curly one .
Who teach you to do it ? ''
ask Bab , a she and Betty walk up and down admire them .
`` Horse blanket , '' answer Ben , soberly .
`` What ! ''
cry both girl , stop to stare .
`` Our horse all have their name on their blanket , and I use to copy 'em .
The wagon have sign , and I learn to read that way after father teach me my letter off the red and yellow poster .
First word I know be lion , 'cause I be always goin ' to see old Jubal in his cage .
Father be real proud when I read it right off .
I can draw one , too . ''
Ben proceed to depict an animal intend to represent his lose friend ; but Jubal would not have recognize his portrait , since it look much more like Sancho than the king of the forest .
The child admire it immensely , however , and Ben give them a lesson in natural history which be so interesting that it keep them busy and happy till bedtime ; for the boy describe what he have see in such lively language , and illustrate in such a droll way , it be no wonder they be charm .
CHAPTER VII NEW FRIENDS TROT IN Next day Ben run off to his work with Quackenbos 's `` Elementary History of the United States '' in his pocket , and the Squire 's cow have ample time to breakfast on way-side grass before they be put into their pasture .
Even then the pleasant lesson be not end , for Ben have an errand to town ; and all the way he read busily , tumble over the hard word , and leave bit which he do not understand to be explain at night by Bab .
At `` The First Settlements '' he have to stop , for the schoolhouse be reach , and the book must be return .
The maple-tree closet be easily find , and a little surprise hide under the flat stone ; for Ben pay two stick of red and white candy for the privilege of take book from the new library .
When recess come , great be the rejoicing of the child over their unexpected treat , for Mrs. Moss have few penny to spare for sweet , and , somehow , this candy taste particularly nice , buy out of grateful Ben 's solitary dime .
The little girl share their goody with their favorite mate , but say nothing about the new arrangement , fear it would be spoil if generally know .
They tell their mother , however , and she give them leave to lend their book and encourage Ben to love learn all they could .
She also propose that they should drop patch-work , and help her make some blue shirt for Ben .
Mrs. Barton have give her the material , and she think it would be an excellent lesson in needle-work as well a a useful gift to Ben , -- who , boy-like , never trouble himself a to what he should wear when his one suit of clothes give out .
Wednesday afternoon be the sewing time ; so the two little B 's work busily at a pair of shirt-sleeves , sit on their bench in the doorway , while the rusty needle creak in and out , and the childish voice sing school-songs , with frequent stoppage for lively chatter .
For a week , Ben work away bravely , and never shirk nor complain , although Pat put many a hard or disagreeable job upon him , and chores grow more and more distasteful .
His only comfort be the knowledge that Mrs. Moss and the Squire be satisfy with him ; his only pleasure the lesson he learn while drive the cow , and recite in the evening when the three child meet under the lilac to `` play school . ''
He have no thought of study when he begin , and hardly know that he be do it a he pore over the different book he take from the library .
But the little girl try him with all they Possessed , and he be mortify to find how ignorant he be .
He never own it in word , but gladly accept all the bit of knowledge they offer from their small store ; get Betty to hear him spell `` just for fun ; '' agree to draw Bab all the bear and tiger she want if she would show him how to do sum on the flag , and often beguile his lonely labor by try to chant the multiplication table a they do .
When Tuesday night come round , the Squire pay him a dollar , say he be `` a likely boy , '' and might stay another week if he choose .
Ben thank him and think he would ; but the next morning , after he have put up the bar , he remain sit on the top rail to consider his prospect , for he felt uncommonly reluctant to go back to the society of rough Pat .
Like most boy , he hat work , unless it be of a sort which just suit him ; then he could toil like a beaver and never tire .
His wander life have give him no habit of steady industry ; and , while he be an unusually capable lad of his age , he dearly love to `` loaf '' about and have a good deal of variety and excitement in his life .
Now he saw nothing before him but day of patient and very uninteresting labor .
He be heartily sick of weed ; even rid Duke before the cultivator have lose it charm , and a great pile of wood lay in the Squire 's yard which he know he would be set to pile up in the shed .
Strawberry-picking would soon follow the asparagus cultivation ; then hay ; and and so on all the long bright summer , without any fun , unless his father come for him .
On the other hand , he be not oblige to stay a minute longer unless he like .
With a comfortable suit of clothes , a dollar in his pocket , and a row of dinner-baskets hang in the school-house entry to supply him with provision if he do n't mind steal them , what be easy than to run away again ?
Tramping have it charm in fair weather , and Ben have live like a gypsy under canvas for year ; so he fear nothing , and begin to look down the leafy road with a restless , wistful expression , a the temptation grow strong and strong every minute .
Sancho seem to share the longing , for he keep run off a little way and stop to frisk and bark ; then rush back to sit watch his master with those intelligent eye of his , which seem to say , `` Come on , Ben , let u scamper down this pleasant road and never stop till we be tire . ''
Swallows dart by , white cloud flee before the balmy west wind , a squirrel ran along the wall , and all thing seem to echo the boy 's desire to leave toil behind and roam away as care-free a they .
One thing restrain him , the thought of his seem ingratitude to good Mrs. Moss , and the disappointment of the little girl at the loss of their two new play-fellows .
While he pause to think of this , something happen which keep him from do what he would have be sure to regret afterward .
Horses have always be his best friend , and one come trot up to help him now ; though he do not know how much he owe it till long after .
Just in the act of swing himself over the bar to take a shortcut across the field , the sound of approach hoof , unaccompanied by the roll of wheel , catch his ear ; and , pause , he watch eagerly to see who be come at such a pace .
At the turn of road , however , the quick trot stop , and in a moment a lady on a bay mare come pace slowly into sight , -- a young and pretty lady , all in dark blue , with a bunch of dandelion like yellow star in her button-hole , and a silver-handled whip hang from the pommel of her saddle , evidently more for ornament than use .
The handsome mare limp a little , and shake her head a if something plague her ; while her mistress lean down to see what be the matter , say , a if she expect an answer of some sort , -- `` Now , Chevalita , if you have get a stone in your foot , I shall have to get off and take it out .
Why do n't you look where you step , and save me all this trouble ? ''
`` I 'll look for you , ma'am ; I 'd like to ! ''
say an eager voice so unexpectedly , that both horse and rider start a a boy come down the bank with a jump .
`` I wish you would .
You need not be afraid ; Lita be as gentle a a lamb , '' answer the young lady , smile , a if amuse by the boy 's earnestness .
`` She 's a beauty , any way , '' mutter Ben , lift one foot after another till he find the stone , and with some trouble get it out .
`` That be nicely do , and I 'm much oblige .
Can you tell me if that cross-road lead to the Elms ? ''
ask the lady , a she go slowly on with Ben beside her .
`` No , ma'am ; I 'm new in these part , and I only know where Squire Morris and Mrs. Moss live . ''
`` I want to see both of them , so suppose you show me the way .
I be here long ago , and think I should remember how to find the old house with the elm avenue and the big gate , but I do n't . ''
`` I know it ; they call that place the Laylocks now , 'cause there 's a hedge of 'em all down the path and front wall .
It 's a real pretty place ; Bab and Betty play there , and so do I. '' Ben could not restrain a chuckle at the recollection of his first appearance there , and , a if his merriment or his word interest her , the lady say pleasantly , `` Tell me all about it .
Are Bab and Betty your sister ? ''
Quite forget his intended tramp , Ben plunge into a copious history of himself and new-made friend , lead on by a kind look , an inquiring word , and sympathetic smile , till he have tell every thing .
At the school-house corner he stop and say , spread his arm like a sign-post , -- `` That 's the way to the Laylocks , and this be the way to the Squire 's . ''
`` As I 'm in a hurry to see the old house , I 'll go this way first , if you will be kind enough to give my love to Mrs. Morris , and tell the Squire Miss Celia be come to dine with him .
I wo n't say good-by , because I shall see you again . ''
With a nod and a smile , the young lady canter away , and Ben hurry up the hill to deliver his message , feeling a if something pleasant be go to happen ; so it would be wise to defer run away , for the present at least .
At one o'clock Miss Celia arrive , and Ben have the delight of help Pat stable pretty Chevalita ; then , his own dinner hastily eaten , he fell to work at the detested wood-pile with sudden energy ; for a he work he could steal peep into the dining-room , and see the curly brown head between the two gay one , a the three sit round the table .
He could not help hear a word now and then , a the window be open , and these bit of conversation fill him with curiosity for the name `` Thorny , '' `` Celia , '' and `` George '' be often repeat , and an occasional merry laugh from the young lady sound like music in that usually quiet place .
When dinner be over , Ben 's industrious fit leave him , and he leisurely trundle his barrow to and fro till the guest depart .
There be no chance for him to help now , since Pat , anxious to get whatever trifle might be offer for his service , be quite devote in his attention to the mare and her mistress , till she be mount and off .
But Miss Celia do not forget her little guide , and , spy a wistful face behind the wood-pile , pause at the gate and beckon with that win smile of hers .
If ten Pats have stand scowl in the way , Ben would have defy them all ; and , vault over the fence , he run up with a shin face , hop she want some last favor of him .

`` No , miss , I never go to school . ''
`` That be not taught there .
I 'll bring you a book all about it when I come back .
Mr. Gulliver go to the horse-country and hear the dear thing speak their own tongue . ''

She be ` most a kind and ride ` most as well .
Wonder where she 's goin ' to .

Bab and Betty meantime have have a most exciting day ; for when they go home at noon they find the pretty lady there , and she have talk to them like an old friend , give them a ride on the little horse , and kiss them both good-by when they go back to school .

`` I 'm in such a hurry to see the peacock and hear them scream .
She say they do , and that we 'd laugh when old Jack brayed , '' cry Bab , hop about on one foot to work off her impatience .

`` Oh , she 's to be up at the Squire 's till thing be fix , and you be to bring her down .

I know we can , Miss Celia be so kind , '' begin Betty , who care for these thing more than for scream peacock and comical donkey .

`` You 'd better begin to pick up your dud right away , for she wo n't want them clutter round her front yard .

CHAPTER VIII MISS CELIA 'S MAN Ben be not too tired , and the clearing-up begin that very night .
None too soon , for in a day or two thing arrive , to the great delight of the child , who consider move a most interesting play .
First come the phaeton , which Ben spend all his leisure moment in admire ; wonder with secret envy what happy boy would ride in the little seat up behind , and beguile his task by plan how , when he get rich , he would pass his time drive about in just such an equipage , and invite all the boy he meet to have a ride .
Then a load of furniture come creak in at the lodge gate , and the girl have rapture over a cottage piano , several small chair , and a little low table , which they pronounce just the thing for them to play at .
The live stock appear next , create a great stir in the neighborhood , for peacock be rare bird there ; the donkey 's bray startle the cattle and convulse the people with laughter ; the rabbit be continually get out to burrow in the newly make garden ; and Chevalita scandalize old Duke by dance about the stable which he have inhabit for year in stately solitude .

so unexpectedly , that Bab nearly spill the new milk she carry , Betty give such a start that the fresh-laid egg quite skip in the dish , and Ben 's face break into a broad grin over the armful of clover which he bring for the bunny , a he bob his head , say briskly , -- `` She 's all right , miss , Lita be ; and I can bring her over any minute you say . ''
`` I shall want her at four o'clock .

`` Oh , yes , 'm , we 'd love to come !
and we 'll bring our best thing . ''

Grandpa live here then , and we have fine time ; but now they be all go except u two . ''

and you shall be my little sister .

It take so little to make a child happy , it be a pity grown people do not oftener remember it and scatter little bit of pleasure before the small people , a they throw crumb to the hungry sparrow .

He could n't resist an affable nod to Bab and Betty , for they stand under the maple-tree , and the memory of their circulating library make him forget his dignity in his gratitude .

`` My brother have be ill , and I have bring him here to get well .

`` I guess I would ! ''
ejaculate Ben , so heartily that no further assurance be need , and Miss Celia go on , well pleased : -- `` You see , poor Thorny be weak and fretful , and do not like to exert himself , though he ought to be out a great deal , and keep from thinking of his little trouble .

CHAPTER IX A HAPPY TEA Exactly five minute before six the party arrive in great state , for Bab and Betty wear their best frock and hair-ribbons , Ben have a new blue shirt and his shoe on a full-dress , and Sancho 's curl be nicely brush , his frill as white a if just do up .

No wonder the dog sniffed and the child smile , for there be a noble display of little tart and cake , little biscuit and sandwich , a pretty milk-pitcher shape like a white calla rise out of it green leaf , and a jolly little tea-kettle sing away over the spirit-lamp as cosily a you please .

A pretty , well-dressed child of six , with dark hair cut short across the brow , a rosy face , a stout pair of leg , leave bare by the sock which have slip down over the dusty little shoe .

Before Miss Celia could speak , the stranger calmly announce his mission .
`` I have come to see the peacock . ''
`` You shall presently -- '' begin Miss Celia , but get no further , for the child add , come a step nearer , -- `` And the wabbits . ''
`` Yes , but first wo n't you -- '' `` And the curly dog , '' continue the small voice , a another step bring the resolute young personage nearer .
`` There he be . ''

`` Certainly , if he will . ''
`` And the peacock scream . ''
`` Any thing more , sir ? ''

with a glance at the new shoe which cause them to creak uneasily .

He have see a good many fine lady in his life ; and those he have know have be very gay in the color of their hat and gown , very fond of cheap jewelry , and much give to feather , lace , and furbelow ; so it rather puzzle him to discover why Miss Celia look so sweet and elegant in such a simple suit .
He do not then know that the charm be in the woman , not the clothes ; or that merely live near such a person would do more to give him gentle manner , good principle , and pure thought , than almost any other training he could have have .
But he be conscious that it be pleasant to be there , neatly dress , in good company , and go to church like a respectable boy .
